The Triangle Inequality says that the sum of the lengths of any two sides must be greater than the length of the third side. That means $8+8=16$ must be greater than the length of the third side. The third side has a whole number length, so the greatest possible length is 15 units. That makes the perimeter $8+8+15=\boxed{31}$ units.

Extracting Answer from: 
By the Triangle Inequality, the length of the third side must be less than the sum of the first two sides ($8+8=16$) and greater than the difference of the first two sides ($8-8=0$), so the length of the third side must be an integer between $1$ and $15$, inclusive. 

For the greatest possible perimeter, we want the third side to be as large as possible. Since $15$ is the largest possible length, we check if it's a valid side length using the Triangle Inequality: 

$8+8>15$? Yes 

$8+15>8$? Yes 

$15+8>8$? Yes 

Since all three inequalities hold, the largest possible perimeter is $8+8+15 = \boxed{31}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
We recognize 7, 24, and 25 as a Pythagorean triple. That means the area of the triangle is $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 7 \cdot 24 = 84.$ The altitudes are then easy to find: $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{7},$ $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{24},$ and $\frac{2 \cdot 84}{25}.$ Then, our answer is $\frac{168}{7} + \frac{168}{24} + \frac{168}{25} = \boxed{37.72}.$

Extracting Answer from: 
Recall that in a triangle with sides $a,$ $b,$ and $c,$ the area of the triangle is $K = \frac{1}{2}ab\sin C$.  Then the length of the altitude drawn to side $a$ is $h_a = \frac{2K}{a}$, and similarly for the other altitudes.

Using the formula $K = \frac{1}{2} ab\sin C$ with $a = 24$ and $b = 7$ gives $K = 84\sin C$, so $h_a = \frac{2K}{a} = \frac{2}{3}\cdot 84 \sin C = 56 \sin C$ .  Similarly, $h_b = 24 \sin A$ and $h_c = 7 \sin B$.

We can find $\sin A$ and $\sin B$ using the Pythagorean identity
\[\sin^2 A + \cos^2 A = 1.\]Since $\angle C$ is a right angle, we have $\sin A = \cos C = \frac{24}{25}$ and $\sin B = \cos A = \frac{7}{25}$.  Then $h_a = 56 \cdot \frac{24}{25}$, $h_b = 24 \cdot \frac{7}{25}$, and $h_c = 7 \cdot 1 = 7$, so $h_a + h_b + h_c = 56 \cdot \frac{24}{25} + 24 \cdot \frac{7}{25} + 7 = \boxed{49.44}$.

Since the measure of angle $BAC$ is 42, the other two equal angles of triangle $ABC$ must be $(180 - 42)/2 = 138/2 = 69$ degrees each. We will introduce the point $F$, which is the center of the circle, and draw segments from each vertex to $F$. Since segment $ED$ is tangent to the circle at point $C$, it must be perpendicular to radius $CF$. Angle $BAC$ is bisected by segment $AF$, so angle $FAC$ is 21 degrees. Angle $FCA$ is also 21 degrees since triangle $AFC$ is isosceles. Thus, the measure of angle $ACD$ is $90 - 21 = \boxed{69\text{ degrees}}$, which is the same as the two base angles of triangle ABC. Extracting Answer from: 
Since $AC = AB$, angles $BAC$ and $BCA$ are congruent, and so $\angle BCA = 69^\circ$. Since $ED$ is tangent to the circle, we have $\angle ECD = \angle BCA$, so $\angle ECD = 69^\circ$. Finally, angle $ACD$ is supplementary to angle $ECD$, so $\angle ACD = 180^\circ - \angle ECD = \boxed{111}$ degrees.

Extracting Answer from: 
A circular sheet of paper with radius 6 inches has a circumference of $2\pi r = 2 \pi (6) = 12\pi$ inches. Since this sheet of paper is cut into 3 equal sectors, each sector must have an arc length of $1/3$ that of the original circle, so each sector has an arc length of $4\pi$ inches. Because of the way that a cone is formed, the original radius of the circle now becomes the slant height of the cone and the arc length becomes the perimeter of the cone's base. If we let the radius of the cone base be $R$, then we know that the circumference $4\pi$ must equal $2\pi R$, so $2 \pi R = 4 \pi$. Dividing both sides of this equation by $2\pi$, we get that the radius of the cone base is $2$ inches.

We then notice that the radius of the base, the altitude of the cone, and the slant height of the cone form a right triangle where the slant height is the hypotenuse. Since we know both the radius and the slant height, we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find the height $h$ of the cone. We have that $2^2 + h^2 = 6^2$, so $h^2=32$. Taking the square root of both sides, we have that $h=\sqrt{32}=\boxed{4\sqrt{2}}$ inches.

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/geometry/answers/1081_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
When a sector is formed, the circumference of the circle (2πr) is equal to the circumference of the cone's base (which is just the length of the sector's arc).

The total circumference of the circle is 2π(6) = 12π inches. Dividing that into 3 equal sectors means each sector has an arc length of 4π inches.

When forming a cone, the arc length becomes the base circumference of the cone, so each cone's base has a circumference of 4π inches. We know that C = 2πr for a circle, and for a cone C = πd, so in this case, 4π = πd. Solving for d, we get 4 inches.

Therefore, the radius of each cone is r = d/2 = 2 inches.

The slant height of each cone is the radius of the original circle (6 inches), and the height of each cone can be found using the Pythagorean Theorem: h^2 = (6^2 - 2^2) = 32, so h = sqrt(32) = 4*sqrt(2) inches.

Therefore, the height of each cone is 4*sqrt(2) inches.

Let $r$ be the radius of the base of the cylinder and let $h$ be the height of the cylinder. The volume $V$ is given by the formula $V=\pi r^2 h$. Substituting the given values, we get $$2700\pi = \pi (30^2) h.$$ Solving for $h$, we get $$h = \frac{2700\pi}{900\pi} = 3.$$ Therefore, the height of the tank is $\boxed{3\text{ meters}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Since $E$ is the midpoint of $BC$, $BE=EC$. Since triangles $\triangle ABE$ and $\triangle AEC$ have equal base length and share the same height, they have the same area.

$\triangle ABC$ has $\frac{1}{2}$ the area of the rectangle, so the white triangle, $\triangle AEC$, has $1/4$ the area of the rectangle.

Hence the shaded region has $1 - \frac{1}{4}=\frac{3}{4}$ of the area of the rectangle, or $\boxed{75} \%$.

Extracting Answer from: 
The rectangle is four times as long as it is wide, so the sides are in a ratio of $4:1$. Let the width of the rectangle be $x$, so the length of the rectangle is $4x$. Thus, $AB=x$ and $CB=3x$. 

Since $E$ is the midpoint of $\overline{BC}$, $BE = CE = \frac{3x}{2}.$ The shaded region can be split into two triangles that are congruent to each other. Each triangle has an area of $\frac{1}{2} \cdot x \cdot \frac{3x}{2} = \frac{3x^2}{4}$. The total shaded region therefore has an area of $2\left(\frac{3x^2}{4}\right) = \frac{3x^2}{2}.$ The area of the whole rectangle is $x\cdot 4x = 4x^2$.

The ratio of the shaded area to the whole area of the rectangle is: 

$$\frac{\frac{3x^2}{2}}{4x^2} = \frac{3}{8}.$$ 

So, the shaded region is $\boxed{37.5\%}$ of the whole rectangle.

Reflecting the point (0,0) over the vertical line $x=1$ produces the point $(2,0)$.

[asy]
unitsize(0.5 cm);

pair A=(0,0),B=(2,0),C=(4,0),D=(2,2),E=(2,-2);

draw((-2,0)--(6,0));
draw((0,-3)--(0,3));
draw((1,-3)--(1,3),dashed);
draw((0,2)--(6,2),dashed);

dot("$A$",A,dir(225));
dot("$B$",B,SE);
[/asy]

Reflecting $(2,0)$ over the line $y=2$ results in $(2,4)$.

[asy]
unitsize(0.5 cm);

pair A=(0,0),B=(2,0),C=(4,0),D=(2,2),E=(2,-2),F=(2,4);

draw((-2,0)--(6,0));
draw((0,-3)--(0,6));
draw((1,-3)--(1,6),dashed);
draw((0,2)--(6,2),dashed);
draw((2,0)--(2,6),dashed);

dot("$A$",A,dir(225));
dot("$B$",B,SE);
dot("$F$",F,NE);
[/asy]

Thus, the resulting point is $\boxed{(2,4)}$.

For $\triangle ABC$ to be acute, all angles must be acute.

For $\angle A$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie above the line passing through $A$ and perpendicular to $\overline{AB}$. The segment of that line in the first quadrant lies between $P(4,0)$ and $Q(0, 4)$.

For $\angle B$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie below the line through $B$ and perpendicular to $\overline{AB}$. The segment of that line in the first quadrant lies between $S(14,0)$ and $T(0, 14)$.

For $\angle C$ to be acute, point $C$ must lie outside the circle $U$ that has $\overline{AB}$ as a diameter.

We "complete" the truncated cone by adding a smaller, similar cone atop the cut, forming a large cone.  We don't know the height of the small cone, so call it $x$.  Since the small and large cone are similar, we have $x/4=(x+6)/8$; solving yields $x=6$.  Hence the small cone has radius 4, height 6, and volume $(1/3)\pi(4^2)(6)=32\pi$ and the large cone has radius 8, height 12, and volume $(1/3)\pi(8^2)(12)=256\pi$.  The solid's volume is the difference of these two volumes, or $256\pi-32\pi=224\pi$ cubic cm.  Thus we see $n=\boxed{224}$.

The volume of this truncated cone is equal to the difference of the volumes of the larger and smaller cones that make it up. The volume of a cone with radius $r$ and height $h$ is $\frac{1}{3}\pi r^2 h$, so the volume of the larger cone is $\frac{1}{3}\pi (8^2)(6)$ and the volume of the smaller cone is $\frac{1}{3}\pi (4^2)(6)$. Therefore, the volume of the truncated cone is \[\frac{1}{3}\pi(8^2)(6)-\frac{1}{3}\pi(4^2)(6)=4\pi(32)=128\pi.\]So $n=\boxed{128}$.

Since $E$ is the midpoint of $AC$, the area of triangle $BCE$ is half the area of triangle $ABC$, or $144/2 = 72$.

[asy]
import geometry;

unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, G, M, N;

A = (1,3);
B = (0,0);
C = (4,0);
D = (B + C)/2;
E = (C + A)/2;
F = (A + B)/2;
G = (A + B + C)/3;
M = extension(G, G + B - C, A, B);
N = extension(G, G + B - C, A, C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--D);
draw(B--E);
draw(C--F);
draw(M--N);

label("$A$", A, dir(90));
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$G$", G, SSW);
label("$M$", M, NW);
label("$N$", N, NE);
[/asy]

Since $GN$ is parallel to $BC$, triangles $ENG$ and $ECB$ are similar.  Furthermore, $G$ is the centroid of triangle $ABC$, so the ratio of similarity is $EG/EB = 1/3$.  Therefore, the area of triangle $ENG$ is $72 \cdot (1/3)^2 = \boxed{8}$.

[asy]
pair A,B,C,D,G,K,S,T;
A=(4,0);
B=(0,3);
C=(5,6);
D=(B+C)/2;
G=(A+B+C)/3;
K=(G.x,0);
S=extension(G,K,A,B);
T=extension(G,K,A,C);
draw(A--B--C--cycle,linewidth(0.7));
draw(D--G,linewidth(0.7));
draw(K--G,linewidth(0.7));
draw(S--K--T,linewidth(0.7));
label("$A$",A,SE);
label("$B$",B,W);
label("$C$",C,NE);
label("$D$",D,NW);
label("$G$",G,NE);
label("$K$",K,S);
label("$M$",S,W);
label("$N$",T,NE);
draw(rightanglemark(K,D,G,2));
[/asy]

Let the line through $G$ parallel to $BC$ intersect $AB$ and $AC$ at $M$ and $N$, respectively.  Then $MB = \frac{1}{3}AB$ and $NC = \frac{1}{3}AC$, so $$MN = AB - AM - NC = AB - \frac{1}{3}AB - \frac{1}{3}AC = \frac{1}{3}AB + \frac{2}{3}BC.$$Since the line $BC$ is parallel to the line $MN$, the height of triangle $ABC$ from $A$ is parallel to the height of triangle $AMN$ from $A$, so the ratio of their areas is equal to the ratio of their bases.  Hence $$\frac{[AMN]}{[ABC]} = \frac{AM \cdot AN}{AB \cdot AC} = \frac{1}{9}.$$We can compute that $AD = \frac{2}{3}GD$, so $GD = \frac{3}{2}AD$.  By the Pythagorean Theorem on right triangle $GAD$, we have $$\left(\frac{3}{2}AD\right)^2 = AG^2 - \left(\frac{1}{3}AD\right)^2 = \frac{4}{9}AG^2.$$Hence $AG = \frac{2}{3}GD = AD$.  Similarly, $BG = BD$ and $CG = CD$, so triangle $BDC$ is isosceles, and $$\angle NCE = \angle BCA = \angle BAM.$$Since $BG = BD$, triangles $BGN$ and $BDP$ are congruent, where $P$ is the projection of $B$ onto $AC$.  Hence $[BGN] = \frac{1}{2}[BDP]$.  Similarly, $[AMC] = \frac{1}{2}[CEM]$.  Therefore, \begin{align*}
[ENG] &= [ABC] - [AMN] - [BGN] \\
&= [ABC] - \frac{1}{9}[ABC] - \frac{1}{2}\left([BDP] + [CEM]\right) \\
&= \frac{1}{2} [ABC] - \frac{1}{2}[BDP] - \frac{1}{2}[CEM] \\
&= \frac{1}{2}\left([ABC] - [BDC]\right) \\
&= \frac{1}{2}\left(144 - \frac{1}{2}(BC)(AD)\right) \\
&= \frac{1}{2}\cdot 60 = \boxed{30}.
\end{align*}

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/geometry/answers/174_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Let $r$ be the radius of the sphere and $h$ be the height of the box. Then, the diameter of the sphere is equal to $h$, so $2r=h$. Also, the diagonal of the box is equal to the diameter of the sphere, so $30\sqrt{3}=2r=h$. Solving for $r$ gives $r=15\sqrt{3}$.

The volume of the sphere is $\frac{4}{3}\pi r^3=\frac{4}{3}\pi(15\sqrt{3})^3 = 4\cdot5^3\pi^3$. The volume of the box is $30^3$. Since the sphere fits snugly inside the box, the volume that is not occupied by the sphere is equal to the volume of the box minus the volume of the sphere. Thus, the answer is \[30^3 - 4\cdot5^3\pi^3 = \boxed{261000}\text{ cubic centimeters}.\]

When a point is reflected over the $y$-axis, its $x$-coordinate changes sign. That means $(a,b)$ reflects to the point $(-a,b)$. Since $(a,b)$ reflects to $(j,k)$, we have $(-a, b) = (j,k)$. Using the fact that $a+j = 0$, we know $j = -a$. Therefore, we have $(-a,b) = (j,k) = (-a,k)$, which implies $b = k$.

Extracting Answer from: 
The surface area of the cube is $6s^2$.  The volume of the cube is $s^3$, and five times its edge length is $5s$.

Thus, we have the equation $6s^2 = s^3 + 5s$.  Dividing both sides by $s$ (which we can do since $s > 0$), we have $6s = s^2 + 5$.  Rearranging, we have the quadratic $s^2 - 6s + 5 = 0$, which factors as $(s - 1)(s - 5) = 0$.  Thus, the possible values for $s$ are $s = 1$ and $s = 5$, and their sum is $\boxed{6}$.

Let's first draw the two lines $2x+y=4$ and $x+y=1$ on the coordinate plane so we can see the quadrilateral region that satisfies all of the inequalities. 

[asy]
Fill((0,0)--(0,1)--(1,-1)--(2,0)--cycle,gray(.7));
Draw((0,4)--(2,-2), red);
Draw((-2,1) -- (4,-3),Dotted);
Draw((0,1)--(1,-1),blue);
label("$2x+y=4$",(0,4),NE);
label("$x+y=1$",(2,-2),SE);
label("$y=-2x+1$",(-1,-1),SW);
label("$y=-2x+4$",(2,3),NE);
label("$x$",(4,0),E);
label("$y$",(0,4),N);
[/asy]

The solutions to $2x+y \le 4$ form a region below the line $2x+y=4$, including the line itself. The solutions to $x+y \ge 1$ form a region above the line $x+y=1$, including the line itself. Finally, $x \ge 0$ and $y \ge 0$ specify the first quadrant. These four conditions together tell us that the solutions occur inside the shaded parallelogram: 

[asy]
Fill((0,0)--(0,1)--(1,-1)--(2,0)--cycle,gray(.7));
Draw((0,4)--(2,-2), red);
Draw((-2,1) -- (4,-3),Dotted);
Draw((0,1)--(1,-1),blue);
Draw((0,0)--(2,0)--(1,-1)--(0,1)--cycle);
label("$2x+y=4$",(0,4),NE);
label("$x+y=1$",(2,-2),SE);
label("$y=-2x+1$",(-1,-1),SW);
label("$y=-2x+4$",(2,3),NE);
label("$x$",(4,0),E);
label("$y$",(0,4),N);
[/asy]

The vertices of this parallelogram can be found by solving various intersection problems. For example, $2x+y=4$ and $x+y=1$ intersect at $(-1, 3)$, and only the first quadrant solution $x \ge 0$ and $y \ge 0$ is valid. Continuing in this fashion, we can find that the vertices of the parallelogram are $(-1,3)$, $(0,1)$, $(1,-1)$, and $(2,0)$. We can re-order these so that the consecutive vertices form line segments in the same direction: 

[asy]
Fill((0,0)--(0,1)--(1,-1)--(2,0)--cycle,gray(.7));
Draw((0,4)--(2,-2), red);
Draw((-2,1) -- (4,-3),Dotted);
Draw((0,1)--(1,-1),blue);
Draw((0,0)--(2,0)--(1,-1)--(0,1)--cycle);
label("$2x+y=4$",(0,4),NE);
label("$x+y=1$",(2,-2),SE);
label("$y=-2x+1$",(-1,-1),SW);
label("$y=-2x+4$",(2,3),NE);
label("$x$",(4,0),E);
label("$y$",(0,4),N);

pair[] A={(0,1), (1,-1), (2,0), (-1,3)}; 
for(int i=0; i<=3; ++i)
  {
    draw(A[i]--A[(i+1)%4], Arrow(6));
    label("$\ell_"+string(i+1)+"$", A[i]--A[(i+1)%4], dir(A[(i+1)%4]-A[i])*CCW);
  }
[/asy]

The first segment we intersect traverses from $(0,1)$ to $(1,-1)$ and goes in a northwest direction, so it has slope $(-1-1)/(1-0)=-2$. Thus it models the line $y=-2x+b$ for some $b$. Plugging in $(1,-1)$, we see that $b=1$. Therefore, $\ell_1$ has equation $y=-2x+1$. 

Similarly, we can find that the third segment $\ell_3$ goes in a southeast direction with slope $0.5$ from $(1,-1)$ to $(2,0)$, so $\ell_3$ has equation $y=0.5x-1$. 

The second segment $\ell_2$ goes from $(1,-1)$ to $(-1,3)$, so it has slope $(3-(-1))/(-1-1)=-1$. Thus its equation is $y=-x+2$. Finally, the fourth segment $\ell_4$ goes from $(-1,3)$ back to $(0,1)$, so it has slope $(-1-3)/(0-(-1))=4$. Therefore, its equation is $y=4x+5$. 

We want to find the length of the longest side. The four sides have lengths $\sqrt{2}$, $\sqrt{10}$, $\sqrt{2}$, and $\sqrt{5}$. Thus the longest side has length $\sqrt{10}$. 

Therefore, the answer is $10+1=\boxed{11}$.

$\triangle DBC$ has base $\overline{BC}$ of length 8 and height $\overline{BD}$ of length 3; therefore, its area is $\frac{1}{2}\times8\times 3=12$.

The area of quadrilateral $DBEF$ equals the area of $\triangle DBC$ minus the area of $\triangle FEC$.

$\triangle FEC$ has base $EC=BC-BE=8-4=4$. The height of $\triangle FEC$ is equal to the vertical distance from point $F$ to the $x$-axis, which is equal to the $y$-coordinate of point $F$, or 2. Therefore, the area of $\triangle FEC$ is $\frac{1}{2}\times4\times 2=4$.

Finally, the area of quadrilateral $DBEF$ is $12-4=\boxed{8}$.

Since $D$ and $E$ are midpoints, we know that $DE$ is parallel to $AC$.   Changing to an $x,y$ coordinate axis shown, the points $D$ and $E$ have coordinates $(0,3)$ and $(4,0)$, respectively, so the slope of line $DE$ is $\frac{0-3}{4-0}=-\frac{3}{4}$. Also, note that $BD$ is vertical, so its slope is undefined.  Since quadrilateral $DBEF$ is a trapezoid, it has two sets of parallel sides, namely $\overline{DE}$ and $\overline{BF}$, and $\overline{BD}$ and $\overline{EF}$.  Therefore, the height of the trapezoid is the distance between lines $DE$ and $BF$, which is the same as the perpendicular distance from $D$ to line $BF$.

To find the equation of line $BF$, we first find the slope.  The slope of line $BC$ is $\frac{0-6}{8-0}=-\frac{3}{4}$, so the slope of line $BF$ is the negative reciprocal of $BC$, which is $\frac{4}{3}$.  Therefore, line $BF$ has equation $y-0=\frac{4}{3}(x-0)$, or $y=\frac{4}{3}x$.  The perpendicular distance from $D(0,3)$ to line $BF$ is $\frac{|4(0)-3(3)|}{\sqrt{(4)^2+(3)^2}}=\frac{9}{5}$, so the area of trapezoid $DBEF$ is $\frac{1}{2}\cdot3\cdot \frac{9}{5}+\frac{1}{2}\cdot 4\cdot3=\boxed{\frac{39}{5}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
We are going to use a two-step process to find the area of $\triangle QPS$:

1. Find the area of $\triangle PRQ$ using the formula $\frac{1}{2} \cdot PQ \cdot QR \cdot \sin \angle PRQ$.
2. Find the area of $\triangle QPS$ by subtracting the areas of $\triangle PRS$ and $\triangle PRQ$ from the area of $\triangle PQS$.

First, we need to find the length of $PQ$. Note that $\triangle PRQ$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle since $\angle PRQ=120^\circ$ and $\angle PQR=30^\circ$. Therefore, $PQ = 2 \cdot QR = 16$. 

Now, we can find the area of $\triangle PRQ$:

$$\text{Area of } \triangle PRQ = \frac{1}{2} \cdot PQ \cdot QR \cdot \sin \angle PRQ = \frac{1}{2} \cdot 16 \cdot 8 \cdot \sin 120^\circ = 64 \sqrt{3}.$$

Next, we need to find the lengths of $PS$ and $QS$. Since $\triangle RPS$ is a right triangle with legs $RP = 12$ and $RS = 24-8=16$, we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find $PS$:

$$PS^2 = RP^2 + RS^2 = 12^2 + 16^2 = 4\cdot 100 \quad \Rightarrow \quad PS = 20.$$

Similarly, $\triangle SQP$ is a right triangle with legs $SQ = 24-(-8) = 32$ and $QP = 16$, so we can use the Pythagorean Theorem to find $QS$:

$$QS^2 = SQ^2 + QP^2 = 32^2 + 16^2 = 4\cdot 800 \quad \Rightarrow \quad QS = 4\sqrt{50}= 8\sqrt{2}.$$

Now, we can find the area of $\triangle QPS$:

$$\text{Area of } \triangle QPS = \frac{1}{2} \cdot PQ \cdot QS = \frac{1}{2} \cdot 16 \cdot 8\sqrt{2}= 64\sqrt{2}.$$

Finally, we can find the area of $\triangle PRS$:

$$\text{Area of } \triangle PRS = \text{Area of } \triangle PQS - \text{Area of } \triangle PRQ = 64\sqrt{2} - 64\sqrt{3}.$$

Therefore, the area of $\triangle QPS$ is:

$$\text{Area of } \triangle QPS = \text{Area of } \triangle PQS - \text{Area of } \triangle PRS = 128\sqrt{2} - 64\sqrt{2} + 64\sqrt{3} = \boxed{64\sqrt{2} + 64\sqrt{3}}.$$

Since $\angle WAX = 90^\circ$ regardless of the position of square $ABCD$, then $A$ always lies on the semi-circle with diameter $WX$.

The center of this semi-circle is the midpoint, $M$, of $WX$.

To get from $P$ to $M$, we must go up 4 units and to the left 3 units (since $WX=2$), so $PM^2=3^2+4^2=25$ or $PM=5$.

Since the semi-circle with diameter $WX$ has diameter 2, it has radius 1, so $AM=1$.

So we have $AM=1$ and $MP=5$.

Let $A = (0, 0)$, and let the center of the square with side length 6 be $S = (3, 3)$.  We let $P$ have coordinates $(6, -2)$.  We want to find the maximum value of $AP$.

First, we see that $AP = \sqrt{6^2 + 2^2} = 2\sqrt{10}$.  Now consider the point $A'$ reflected across the line $SP$.  The line containing $A'$ and $P$ intersects the side of length 6 at point $P'$, and $AP = A'P'$.  By the Pythagorean theorem,
\[SP' = \sqrt{SP^2 - PP'^2} = \sqrt{3^2 + 2^2} = \sqrt{13},\]so $A'P' = SP' + SA' = 2\sqrt{13} + 3.$

Note that point $A'$ lies within one of the squares of side length 2.  If we take point $A'$ to be the point closest to $P$, we will obtain the maximal possible distance from $A$ to $P$.

[asy]
path square = scale(2) * unitsquare;

draw(square); draw(shift(4) * square); draw(shift(4, 4) * square); draw(shift(0, 4) * square);
draw((2, 0)--(4, 0)); draw((0, 2)--(0, 4)); draw((6, 2)--(6, 4)); draw((2, 6)--(4, 6));

pair a = (1, 3);
pair b = shift(4, 3) * dir(45);
pair c = shift(3, 2) * dir(-45);
pair d = shift(2, 3) * dir(-135);
draw(rightanglemark(shift(3,3),a,b,10));
draw(rightanglemark(shift(3,3),a,d,10));
draw(a--b--c--d--cycle);

pair pa = (6, -2);
pair sa = (3, 3);
pair sp = intersectionpoint(pa--(pa + (a - pa)/abs(a-pa)*abs(sa-pa)), (2, 4)--(4, 6));
draw((6,0)--sp);
draw(Arc(sa, sqrt(13), -atan2(2, 3)*180/pi + 180, -atan2(2, 3)*180/pi + 270), red);

label("$2$", (1, 6), N); label("$2$", (3, 6), N); label("$2$", (5, 6), N);

label("$2$", (6, 5), E); label("$2$", (6, 3), E); label("$2$", (6, 1), E);

label("$W$", (2, 4), NW); label("$X$", (4, 4), NE); label("$Y$", (4, 2), SE); label("$Z$", (2, 2), SW);
dot("$A$", a, NE); label("$B$", b, E); label("$C$", c, S); label("$D$", d, W); dot("$P$", pa, ESE);
dot("$A'$", (0, 3), NE, red); dot("$S$", sa, N); dot("$P'$", sp, NE);
[/asy]

Then $AA' = 1$, so
\[AP = A'P' - AA' = 2\sqrt{13} + 3 - 1 = 2\sqrt{13} + 2.\][asy]
path square = scale(2) * unitsquare;

draw(square); draw(shift(4) * square); draw(shift(4, 4) * square); draw(shift(0, 4) * square);

[/asy] Hence, the maximum distance is $\boxed{2\sqrt{13} + 2}$.

We first compute the length of the longer base. Using the relationship between the isosceles trapezoid and two congruent right triangles, we have \[
\frac{5}{2\sqrt{3}}=\tan(30^\circ)=\frac{1}{\sqrt{3}},
\]which simplifies to $5\sqrt{3}/3$. Therefore, the perimeter of the trapezoid is \[
2\cdot(5+5\sqrt{3}/3)+2\cdot\sqrt{(2\sqrt{3})^2+((5\sqrt{3}/3)-5)^2}
\] which simplifies to $20+6\sqrt{21}$ inches.

Another way to find the length of the longer base is to use the fact that the two small triangles are $30-60-90$ triangles, with hypotenuse equal to the longer base $b$. Thus, \[
b = 2 \cdot \frac{5}{\sqrt{3}}= \frac{10 \sqrt{3}}{3}.
\] The perimeter is then $2 \cdot 5 + \frac{5}{\sqrt{3}} + \frac{10 \sqrt{3}}{3} + 2 \cdot 2 \sqrt{3} = 20 + 6 \sqrt{21}$.

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/geometry/answers/418_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
First observe that $AD$ and $BC$ are parallel.  Let the tangents at $A$, $B$, $C$, and $D$ to each circle intersect.  We have an isosceles trapezoid with bases $AD$ and $BC$ and height $2+4=6$.  The area of this trapezoid is thus $\frac{(2+4)(6)}{2}=18$. 

To find the area of the two triangles, note that $\triangle{ABO}$ and $\triangle{DPC}$ are similar and the ratio of the height to base is $1:2$.  Thus, the ratio of their areas is $\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)\left(\frac12\right)=\frac14$.  Call the area of $\triangle{ABO}=a$.  The area of $\triangle{DPC}$ is then $\frac{a}{4}$. 

The area of the concave hexagon can be found by subtracting the area of the two triangles from the area of the trapezoid.  

Thus, the answer is $18-a-\frac{a}{4}=\frac{63}{4}-\frac34 a$. 

It is left to calculate $a$.  Drop the altitude $OM$ and $PN$ from $O$ and $P$, respectively, to segment $CD$.

Further extend $MN$ to meet $AD$ and $BC$ at $E$ and $F$, respectively.  

Because  $OM$ is a radius of circle $O$, $OA=2$, and $AD=6$, we have $AM=\sqrt{6^2-2^2}=2\sqrt{5}$.  

Since the tangents are parallel, $\triangle AMN$ is similar to $\triangle ADF$.  The ratio of their sides is $\frac{MN}{DF}=\frac26=\frac13$.  Hence $MN=\frac23 (AD)=4$.

Similarly, because $PN$ is a radius of circle $P$, $PB=2\sqrt{3}$, and $BC=6$, we have $CN=\sqrt{6^2-12}=2\sqrt{3}$.  

Again, since the tangents are parallel, $\triangle CNP$ is similar to $\triangle BCF$.  The ratio of their sides is $\frac{NP}{CF}=\frac42=2$.  So $NP=2 CF$.  Because $\triangle CDF$ is similar to $\triangle CBP$, the ratio of their perimeters is $6:4\sqrt{3}$.  Thus, $CF=\frac{6}{2+\sqrt{3}}=\frac{12-6\sqrt{3}}{3}=4-2\sqrt{3}$.  Then $NP=8-4\sqrt{3}$.

Finally, we see that $MN+NP=12-4\sqrt{3}$.

The area of $\triangle{ABO}$ is thus $\frac12(2\sqrt{5})(4-2\sqrt{3})=\sqrt{50}-\sqrt{30}$.

Thus, the area of the concave hexagon is $\frac{63}{4}-\frac34a=\frac{63}{4}-\frac{3}{4}(\sqrt{50}-\sqrt{30})=\boxed{\frac{117-3\sqrt{150}}{4}}$.

Since the volume of a sphere is directly proportional to its diameter cubed, the ratio of the volume of the ball with a diameter of 4 cm to the volume of the ball with a diameter of 3 cm is $(4/3)^3
= 64/27$.  Since all of the rubber bands have the same volume, it follows that the number of rubber bands in the ball with diameter 4 is \[ (4/3)^3 \cdot 54 = \frac{64}{27} \cdot 54 = 64 \cdot 2 = 128. \]Therefore the number of rubber bands that Alana needs to add to the ball is $128 - 54 = \boxed{74}$.

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/geometry/answers/432_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
One way to approach this problem is to use the formula for the volume of a sphere, which is V = (4/3)πr^3, where V is the volume, π is pi (approximately 3.14), and r is the radius. Since the diameter of the original ball is 3 cm, the radius is 1.5 cm. The volume of the original ball is therefore:

V1 = (4/3)π(1.5)^3 = 14.14 cubic cm (rounded to two decimal places)

To increase the diameter by 1 cm, we need to add enough rubber bands to increase the radius by 0.5 cm. The new radius will be:

r2 = 1.5 + 0.5 = 2 cm

The new volume will be:

V2 = (4/3)π(2)^3 = 33.51 cubic cm (rounded to two decimal places)

The difference in volume between the two balls is therefore:

ΔV = V2 - V1 = 33.51 - 14.14 = 19.37 cubic cm (rounded to two decimal places)

Since each rubber band has the same volume, we can find the number of rubber bands needed by dividing the volume difference by the volume of one rubber band. Let's assume that each rubber band has a volume of 0.1 cubic cm (this is just an example; you could use a different value if you like). Then the number of rubber bands needed is:

# of rubber bands = ΔV / volume of one rubber band
                  = 19.37 / 0.1
                  = 194 (rounded to the nearest whole number)

The greatest possible perimeter of the isosceles triangle occurs when the remaining side is as long as possible (i.e. the triangle is equilateral). To form an equilateral triangle with the given sides, the length of the remaining side must be equal to the length of the other sides, which is 15 cm. Therefore, the greatest possible perimeter is:

15 cm + 15 cm + 10 cm = 40 cm

So the greatest possible perimeter is 40 cm.

Let the radius be $r$ and the height be $h$. Since the lateral surface area is 3.5 square inches, we have $2\pi rh=3.5$. Since the volume is 3.5 cubic inches, we have $\pi r^2h=3.5$. Dividing the latter equation by the former, we find that $r=\boxed{2}$ inches.

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/geometry/answers/440_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Let $r$ be the radius and let $h$ be the height of the cylinder, both in inches. The lateral surface area of the cylinder is $2\pi rh$, and the volume of the cylinder is $\pi r^2h$. We are given that $2\pi rh=3.5$ and $\pi r^2h=3.5$. Dividing these equations, we find $\frac{2\pi rh}{\pi r^2h}=\frac{2}{r}$, or $\frac{2}{r}=1$. Therefore, $r=2$ inches. Our answer is $\boxed{2}$.

In its initial position, suppose the semi-circle touches the bottom line at $X$, with point $P$ directly above $X$ on the top line. Consider when the semi-circle rocks to the right. [asy]
size(10cm);

// Variables
path semicircle = (-8, 0)--(8, 0){down}..{left}(0, -8){left}..{up}(-8, 0);
real xy = 4 * pi / 3;
pair x = (0, -8); pair p = (0, 4);
pair o = (xy, 0); pair z = (xy, 4); pair y = (xy, -8);

// Drawing
draw((-15, -8)--(15, -8));
draw((-15, 4)--(15, 4));
draw(semicircle, dashed);
draw(x--p, dashed);
draw(shift(xy) * rotate(-30) * semicircle);
draw(z--y);

// labels
label("$Q$", (-4 * sqrt(3) + xy, 4), N);
label("$P$", (0, 4), N);
label("$Z$", (xy, 4), N);
label("$O$", (xy, 0), NE);
label("$X$", (0, -8), S);
label("$Y$", (xy, -8), S);
[/asy] Suppose now the semi-circle touches the bottom line at $Y$ (with $O$ the point on the top of the semi-circle directly above $Y$, and $Z$ the point on the top line directly above $Y$) and touches the top line at $Q$.  Note that $XY=PZ$.

$Q$ is one of the desired points where the semi-circle touches the line above.  Because the diagram is symmetrical, the other point will be the mirror image of $Q$ in line $XP$.  Thus, the required distance is 2 times the length of $PQ$.

Now $PQ=QZ-PZ = QZ-XY$. Since the semi-circle is tangent to the bottom line, and $YO$ is perpendicular to the bottom line and $O$ lies on a diameter, we know that $O$ is the centre of the circle. So $OY=OQ= 8$ cm, since both are radii (or since the centre always lies on a line parallel to the bottom line and a distance of the radius away).

Also, $OZ=4$ cm, since the distance between the two lines is 12 cm. By the Pythagorean Theorem (since $\angle QZO=90^\circ$), then \[ QZ^2 = QO^2 - ZO^2 = 8^2 - 4^2 = 64 - 16 =48\]so $QZ = 4\sqrt{3}$ cm.

Also, since $QZ:ZO = \sqrt{3}:1$, then $\angle QOZ = 60^\circ$.

Thus, the angle from $QO$ to the horizontal is $30^\circ$, so the semi-circle has rocked through an angle of $30^\circ$, ie. has rocked through $\frac{1}{12}$ of a full revolution (if it was a full circle). Thus, the distance of $Y$ from $X$ is $\frac{1}{12}$ of the circumference of what would be the full circle of radius 8, or $XY=\frac{1}{12}(2\pi(8))=\frac{4}{3}\pi$ cm.  (We can think of a wheel turning through $30^\circ$ and the related horizontal distance through which it travels.)

Thus, $PQ = QZ-XY = 4\sqrt{3} - \frac{4}{3}\pi$ cm.

Therefore, the required distance is double this, or $8\sqrt{3}-\frac{8}{3}\pi$ cm or about 5.4788 cm, which is closest to $\boxed{55}$ mm.

Extracting Answer from: 
Let’s first find the distance between the top of the semi-circle and the line it rolls along. If we draw a line from the center of the semi-circle to this line, it will be perpendicular (since we assume it is rolling without slipping).  We then have a right triangle with legs 8 cm and 12 cm. The hypotenuse is then
$$\sqrt{8^2+12^2}=4\sqrt{13},$$
which is the diameter of the semi-circle.  

Consider the picture below; on the left side is a rough illustration of the semi-circle, and on the right is a zoomed-in view of the two points of contact. 

[asy]
import graph;
draw((0,0)--(12,0),Arrows);
draw((0,0)..(8,8/3)..(12,0),Arrows);
label("$12$",(6,-0.6),S);
label("$8$",(4,1));
draw((5.12,-0.8)--(5.12,0.4),EndArrow);
draw((6.88,-0.8)--(6.88,0.4),EndArrow);
draw((5.12,-1.2)--(6.88,-1.2),EndArrow);
label("$4\sqrt{13}$",(2,1.7));
label("$r$",(5.5,-0.5));
[/asy]

Let $r$ be the radius of one of the points of contact.  We draw a line segment from the center of the semi-circle to one of these points of contact. We then have a right triangle, with hypotenuse $4 \sqrt{13}$ and leg $r$. Because this line segment is perpendicular to the line the semi-circle rolls along, all we need to do is subtract the length of the hypotenuse of this right triangle from $2r$, the sum of the two radii.  That is,
\begin{align*}
d &= 2r-2\sqrt{(4\sqrt{13})^2 - r^2} \\
&= 2r-2\sqrt{208-r^2}.
\end{align*}
To finish, we need to solve for $r$, which we can do using the right triangle on the right side of the image above.  Let's label the distance between the two points of contact as $x$ (the quantity we are trying to find).  Then
\[x^2=(2r)^2-(4\sqrt{13})^2\]
(using the Pythagorean Theorem), which simplifies to
\[x^2=4r^2-208.\]
But we can also find $r$ using the Pythagorean Theorem, since the axes of symmetry of the semi-circle, the line above it, and the line the semi-circle rolls along form a right triangle.  The hypotenuse of this triangle has length $4\sqrt{13}+12$, and one leg, which has length equal to $r$, can be found using the Pythagorean Theorem.  We find:
\begin{align*}
(4\sqrt{13}+12)^2 &= (2r)^2 + 12^2 \\
r &= \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{(4\sqrt{13}+12)^2 - 144} \\
&= \frac{1}{2} \sqrt{560 + 96 \sqrt{13}}.
\end{align*}
Plugging this value of $r$ into the formula for $d$, we get
\begin{align*}
d &= 2r-2\sqrt{208-r^2} \\
&= \sqrt{560 + 96 \sqrt{13}} - 2 \sqrt{208 - \frac{1}{4}(560 + 96 \sqrt{13})} \\
&\approx \boxed{391} \text{ millimeters}.
\end{align*}
(Note that we rounded to the nearest whole number as the prompt requested.)

Let $P$ be the point on the unit circle that is $45^\circ$ counterclockwise from $(1,0)$, and let $D$ be the foot of the altitude from $P$ to the $x$-axis, as shown below.

[asy]

pair A,C,P,O,D;

draw((0,-1.2)--(0,1.2),p=black+1.2bp,Arrows(0.15cm));

draw((-1.2,0)--(1.2,0),p=black+1.2bp,Arrows(0.15cm));

A = (1,0);

O= (0,0);

label("$x$",(1.2,0),SE);

label("$y$",(0,1.2),NE);

P = rotate(45)*A;

D = foot(P,A,-A);

draw(O--P--D);

draw(rightanglemark(O,D,P,2));

draw(Circle(O,1));

label("$O$",O,SE);

label("$P$",P,NE);

//label("$A$",A,SE);

label("$D$",D,S);

[/asy]

Triangle $POD$ is a 45-45-90 triangle, so $DO = DP = \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}$.  Therefore, the coordinates of $P$ are $\left(\frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}, \frac{\sqrt{2}}{2}\right)$, so $\tan 45^\circ = \frac{\sin 45^\circ}{\cos 45^\circ} = \frac{\sqrt{2}/2}{\sqrt{2}/2} = \boxed{1}$.

Since $\overline{ADE}$ and $\overline{ABC}$ share a common base and $E$ and $C$ are on the same horizontal line, $$\frac{[\triangle ADE]}{[ABCD]}=\frac{[\triangle ADE]}{[ABCE]}=\frac{AD\cdot DE/2}{AB\cdot AD}=\frac{DE}{2AB}.$$Also, $ABCE$ is a rectangle, so $$\frac{[ABCE]}{[ABCD]}=\frac{AB\cdot BC}{AB\cdot AD}=BC/AD.$$Putting it all together, $$\frac{[\triangle ADE]}{[ABCE]}=\frac{DE}{2AB}\div\frac{BC}{AD}=\frac{DE}{2AB}\cdot \frac{AD}{BC}.$$Since $BC=AE=10$ and $AB=6$, we have $\frac{DE}{2AB}=\frac{10-0}{2\cdot 6}=\frac{5}{6}$ and $\frac{AD}{BC}=2/3$. Multiplying gives us $\frac{5}{6}\cdot \frac{2}{3}=\boxed{\frac{5}{9}}$.

We know that $OA$ and $OB$ are each radii of the semi-circle with center $O$. Thus, $OA=OB=OC+CB=32+36=68$. Therefore, $AC=AO+OC=68+32=100$.

The semi-circle with center $K$ has radius $AK=\frac{1}{2}(AC)=\frac{1}{2}(100)=50$.

The shaded area is equal to the area of the largest semi-circle with center $O$, minus the combined areas of the two smaller unshaded semi-circles with centers $K$ and $M$.  The radius of the smaller unshaded circle is $MB=\frac{1}{2}(CB)=\frac{1}{2}(36)=18$.  Therefore, the shaded area equals \begin{align*}
&\frac{1}{2}\pi(OB)^2-\left(\frac{1}{2}\pi(AK)^2+\frac{1}{2}\pi(MB)^2\right)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(68)^2-\left(\frac{1}{2}\pi(50)^2+\frac{1}{2}\pi(18)^2\right)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(68^2-50^2-18^2)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(4624-2500-324)\\
&=\frac{1}{2}\pi(1800)\\
&=\boxed{900\pi}
\end{align*}

Let $\overline{PQ}$ be a line segment in set $\cal
S$ that is not a side of the square, and let $M$ be the midpoint of $\overline{PQ}$. Let $A$ be the vertex of the square that is on both the side that contains $P$ and the side that contains $Q$. Because $\overline{AM}$ is the median to the hypotenuse of right $\triangle PAQ$, $AM=(1/2)\cdot PQ=(1/2)\cdot2=1$.  Thus every midpoint is 1 unit from a vertex of the square, and the set of all the midpoints forms four quarter-circles of radius 1 and with centers at the vertices of the square.  The area of the region bounded by the four arcs is $4-4\cdot(\pi/4)=4-\pi$, so $100k=100(4-3.14)=\boxed{86}$.

$$\centerline{{\bf OR}}$$Place a coordinate system so that the vertices of the square are at $(0,0)$, $(2,0)$, $(2,2)$, and $(0,2)$. = 1$, and the midpoints of these segments form a quarter-circle with radius 1 centered at the origin.  The set of all the midpoints forms four quarter-circles, and the area of the region bounded by the four arcs is $4-4\cdot(\pi/4)=4-\pi$, so $100k=100(4-3.14)=\boxed{86}$.

Let the hypotenuse $AB$ have length $2x$. Denote the semicircle's radius by $r$. Then the two legs $AC$ and $BC$ each have length $x-r$, and we have $$(x-r)^2+(x-r)^2 = (2r)^2.$$Solving yields $x = \sqrt{2}+1$ and $r = \sqrt{2}-1$. Thus, the area of triangle $ABC$ is $$\frac{1}{2}(\sqrt{2}+1)^2\cdot(\sqrt{2}-1) = \boxed{\frac{3}{2}}.$$

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/geometry/answers/562_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Recall that $\tan$ has period $\pi$, which means it repeats every $\pi$ radians (or every $180^\circ$). Since $\tan 0^\circ = 0$ and $\tan 180^\circ$ is exactly halfway around the unit circle from $\tan 0^\circ$, we have \begin{align*}
\tan 180^\circ &= \tan (180^\circ - 0^\circ) \\
&= -\tan 0^\circ\\&=\boxed{0}.
\end{align*} Note that an equivalent way to get this answer is to use the fact that $\tan$ is an odd function, so $\tan (-x)=-\tan{x}$ for all angles $x$.

Stripped String:

Because of our parallel bases, we can see that corresponding angles of the triangles must be congruent. Therefore, by AA similarity, we see that the two triangles are similar.

If two similar triangles have side ratios of $r : 1,$ the ratio of their areas must be $r^2 : 1.$ In our diagram, we see that the ratio of the sides of the smaller triangle to the sides of the larger triangle is $\dfrac{4\text{ cm}}{10\text{ cm}} = \dfrac{2}{5}.$ Therefore, the ratio of the areas is the square of that, or $\left(\dfrac{2}{5}\right)^2 = \boxed{\dfrac{4}{25}}.$

We start with the formula for the volume of a right circular cone: $$V = \frac{1}{3}\pi r^2h,$$ where $V$ is the volume of the cone, $r$ is the radius of the circular base, and $h$ is the height of the cone. We know that $r = 3\text{ in}$ and $V = 30\pi\text{ in}^3$, so we can solve for $h$: $$30\pi = \frac{1}{3}\pi (3\text{ in})^2 h.$$ Simplifying, we get $$30\pi = 9\pi h,$$ and dividing both sides by $9\pi$, we have $$h = \frac{30\pi}{9\pi} = \frac{10}{3}\text{ in}.$$ Therefore, the cup needs to be $\boxed{\frac{10}{3}\text{ in}}$ tall to hold $30\pi$ cubic inches of water.

We know that in a right triangle, the hypotenuse is a diameter of its circumcircle. Since the sides 8, 15, and 17 form a Pythagorean triple, this triangle is a right triangle with hypotenuse of length 17. Therefore, the diameter of the circle (which is 17) is twice the radius. Solving for the radius, we get:
radius = diameter / 2 = 17 / 2 = 8.5
So the radius of the circle is 8.5 units (to the nearest tenth).

Original File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/geometry/65.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Join $PQ$, $PR$, $PS$, $RQ$, and $RS$. Since the circles with center $Q$, $R$ and $S$ are all tangent to $BC$, then $QR$ and $RS$ are each parallel to $BC$ (as the centres $Q$, $R$ and $S$ are each 1 unit above $BC$). This tells us that $QS$ passes through $R$. When the centers of tangent circles are joined, the line segments formed pass through the associated point of tangency, and so have lengths equal to the sum of the radii of those circles. Therefore, $QR=RS=PR=PS=1+1=2$.

[asy]
size(200);
pair P, Q, R, S;
Q=(0,0);
R=(2,0);
S=(4,0);
P=(3,1.732);
label("Q", Q, SW);
label("R", R, dir(270));
label("S", S, SE);
label("P", P, N);
draw(circle(Q,1), dashed);
draw(circle(P,1), dashed);
draw(circle(R,1), dashed);
draw(circle(S,1), dashed);
draw(P--Q--S--P--R);
[/asy]

Since $PR=PS=RS$, we know $\triangle PRS$ is equilateral, so $\angle PSR=\angle PRS=60^\circ$.  Since $\angle PRS=60^\circ$ and $QRS$ is a straight line, we have $\angle QRP=180^\circ-60^\circ=120^\circ$. Since $QR=RP$, we know $\triangle QRP$ is isosceles, so $$\angle PQR = \frac{1}{2}(180^\circ-120^\circ)= 30^\circ.$$Since $\angle PQS=30^\circ$ and $\angle PSQ=60^\circ$, we have $\angle QPS = 180^\circ - 30^\circ - 60^\circ = 90^\circ$, so $\triangle PQS$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. Thus, the answer is $\boxed{30^\circ}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
We can start by using the identity $\sin (330^\circ) = \sin (360^\circ - 30^\circ)$.  Then, using the formula for $\sin$ of a difference, we get
\[\sin(330^\circ) = \sin 30^\circ = \boxed{\frac{1}{2}}.\]We can also evaluate $\sin 330^\circ$ using the unit circle.  Since $\sin \theta$ gives the $y$-coordinate of the point on the unit circle corresponding to $\theta$, we look for the point on the unit circle that makes an angle of $330^\circ$ with the positive $x$-axis.

[asy]
unitsize(1.5 cm);

draw(Circle((0,0),1));

Extracting Answer from: 
Let the edge length of the cube be $s$. Then the diameter of the sphere is equal to the edge length of the cube, which means the radius of the sphere is $\frac{s}{2}$. The formula for the volume of a sphere is $\frac{4}{3}\pi r^3$, so the volume of the inscribed sphere is $\frac{4}{3}\pi \left(\frac{s}{2}\right)^3 = \frac{\pi}{6}$. Solving for $s$ yields:

\begin{align*}
\frac{4}{3}\pi \left(\frac{s}{2}\right)^3 &= \frac{\pi}{6} \\
\frac{s^3}{8} &= \frac{1}{4} \\
s^3 &= 2 \\
s &= \sqrt[3]{2}
\end{align*}

Therefore, the volume of the cube is $s^3 = \sqrt[3]{2}^3 = 2$ cubic inches. Answer: $\boxed{2}$.

Let the two different sides of the rectangle be $a$ and $b$. Since the perimeter is 26 units, we have the equation $2a+2b=26\Rightarrow a+b=13$. We can rearrange this equation to get $a=13-b$. We want to minimize the value of $\sqrt{a^2+b^2}$. Substituting in the last equation, we have $\sqrt{(13-b)^2+b^2}=\sqrt{169-26b+2b^2}$. This value is minimized when the quadratic $169-26b+2b^2$ is minimized, which occurs when $b$ (which must be an integer) is as close to $-\frac{-26}{2\cdot2}=6.5$ as possible. So let $b=7$ and $a=13-7=6$. So, the shortest possible diagonal is $\sqrt{7^2+6^2}=\boxed{\sqrt{85}}$.

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/geometry/answers/677_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
Let the length and width of the rectangle be $l$ and $w$, respectively. We know that $2l + 2w = 26$, or $l + w = 13$. We want to find the shortest possible length of the diagonal $\sqrt{l^2 + w^2}$.

Since $l+w=13$, we can solve for $w$ to get $w=13-l$. Then the expression for the diagonal becomes $$\sqrt{l^2 + w^2} = \sqrt{l^2 + (13-l)^2}$$ Expanding the square and simplifying gives $\sqrt{2l^2 - 26l + 169}$. Since we want to minimize this expression, we look for the value of $l$ that makes it as small as possible.

Taking the derivative of the expression with respect to $l$ and setting it equal to 0, we get: $$\frac{d}{dl} \sqrt{2l^2 - 26l + 169} = \frac{2l - 13}{\sqrt{2l^2 - 26l + 169}} = 0$$ This gives us $l = \frac{13}{2}$. Since we have an integer parameter, we can take $l=6$ or $l=7$. Checking both possibilities, we find that $l=w=6$ gives us the smallest possible diagonal of $\sqrt{6^2 + 7^2} = \sqrt{85}$.

Therefore, the answer is $\boxed{\sqrt{85}}$.

To save the most rope, we must have $HP$ having minimum length.
For $HP$ to have minimum length, $HP$ must be perpendicular to $CD$. [asy]
pair C, D, H, P;
H=(90,120);
C=(0,0);
D=(140,0);
P=(90,0);
draw(H--C--D--H--P);
label("H", H, N);
label("C", C, SW);
label("D", D, SE);
label("P", P, S);
label("150", (C+H)/2, NW);
label("130", (D+H)/2, NE);
[/asy] (Among other things, we can see from this diagram that sliding $P$ away from the perpendicular position does make $HP$ longer.)
In the diagram, $HC=150$, $HD=130$ and $CD=140$.
Let $HP=x$ and $PD=a$.  Then $CP=140-a$.
By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle HPC$, $x^2 + (140-a)^2 = 150^2$.
By the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle HPD$, $x^2+a^2 = 130^2$.
Subtracting the second equation from the first, we obtain \begin{align*}
(140-a)^2 - a^2 & = 150^2 - 130^2 \\
(19600 - 280a+a^2)-a^2 & = 5600 \\
19600 -280a & = 5600 \\
280a & = 14000 \\
a & = 50
\end{align*} Therefore, $x^2 + 90^2 = 150^2$ or $x^2 = 150^2 - 90^2 = 22500 - 8100 = 14400$ so $x =120$.
So the shortest possible rope that we can use is 120 m, which saves $130+150-120 = \boxed{160}$ m of rope.

We know that:

- The initial cylindrical containers have a radius of 4 cm, which means a diameter of 8 cm (2 × 4 cm).
- The volume of a cylinder is given by V = πr²h, where r is the radius and h is the height.
- We do not know the exact measurements of the height of the containers, but since Sean wants to fill the same amount of sand into the new containers, we can assume that the height remains the same for both sets of containers.
- For the new containers, the radius is 8 cm, which means a diameter of 16 cm (2 × 8 cm).

To calculate the number of new containers Sean needs, we need to compare the volumes of the old and new containers. Since the height is the same for both types of containers, we can ignore h in the volume equation. Then, we get:

- Volume of each old container: V = πr²h = π(4²)h = 16πh
- Volume of each new container: V = πr²h = π(8²)h = 64πh

This tells us that each new container is four times the volume of each old container. Therefore, Sean will need 12 ÷ 4 = 3 new containers to hold all the sand from his old containers. So, Sean needs 3 new containers.

We know that $AG:AD = 2:3$.  Triangles $AMG$ and $ABD$ are similar, so $AM:AB = AG:AD = 2:3$.  Likewise, $AN:AC = AG:AD = 2:3$.

[asy]
import geometry;

unitsize(1 cm);

pair A, B, C, D, E, F, G, M, N;

A = (1,3);
B = (0,0);
C = (4,0);
D = (B + C)/2;
E = (C + A)/2;
F = (A + B)/2;
G = (A + B + C)/3;
M = extension(G, G + B - C, A, B);
N = extension(G, G + B - C, A, C);

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--D);
draw(B--E);
draw(C--F);
draw(M--N);

label("$A$", A, dir(90));
label("$B$", B, SW);
label("$C$", C, SE);
label("$D$", D, S);
label("$E$", E, NE);
label("$F$", F, NW);
label("$G$", G, SSW);
label("$M$", M, NW);
label("$N$", N, NE);
[/asy]

Therefore, the area of triangle $AMN$ is $810 \cdot (2/3)^2 = \boxed{360}$.

Let $[XYZ]$ denote the area of triangle $XYZ$.  Since $G$ is the centroid of triangle $ABC$, we have $AG:GD=BG:GE=CG:GF=2:1$.  Let $x = AG$.  Then we have $GD = \frac{1}{2}x$ and $AD = 3x$.  We also have $\frac{BE}{AE} = \frac{BD}{AD} = \frac{1}{3}$, so $BE = \frac{1}{4}AB$ and $AE = \frac{3}{4}AB$.  It follows that $ME = \frac{1}{4}AB - \frac{1}{3}AM$, so $AM = 3ME$.  Similarly, $AN = 3NF$.  Since the line through $G$ is parallel to $BC$, we have $MN \parallel BC$, so $\triangle AMN \sim \triangle ABC$.  Therefore,
\[\frac{[AMN]}{[ABC]} = \left(\frac{AM}{AB}\right) \left(\frac{AN}{AC}\right) = \frac{9}{16} \cdot \frac{9}{16} = \frac{81}{256}.\]Since $[ABC] = 810$, we have
\[[AMN] = \frac{81}{256} \cdot 810 = \boxed{256}.\]

This triangle's vertices are the intersection points of each pair of lines. The intersection point of $y=0$ and $y=x+4$ is (-4,0). The intersection point of $y=0$ and $x+3y=12$ is (12,0). To find the intersection of the last 2 lines, we substitute the first equation for $y$ and then solve for $x$. Doing so, we get \begin{align*}
x+3y&=12 \\
x+3(x+4)&=12 \\
x+3x+12 &= 12 \\
4x &=0 \\
x &=0
\end{align*} Thus, $y=4$, and the intersection point is (0,4). Let the base of the triangle be the side of the triangle on the $x$-axis. Because this side is between the points (-4,0) and (12,0), its length is $12-(-4)=12+4=16$. The height must be perpendicular to this side and through the final vertex. This is along the $y$-axis. Thus, the height of the triangle is just the $y$-coordinate of the other point, which is 4. Thus, the area of the triangle is $\frac{1}{2} \cdot 16 \cdot 4=8 \cdot 4=\boxed{32} \text{sq units}$.

We can start by sketching the three lines and finding their intersection points:

[asy]
size(6cm);
import TrigMacros;

real xMin = -3;
real xMax = 9;
real yMin = -2;
real yMax = 13;

real m1 = 1;
real b1 = 4;
real m2 = -1/3;
real b2 = 4;

real[] xVals = {xMin, xMax};

pair p1 = (0, b1);
pair p2 = (12/(1 + 3*m1), m1*12/(1 + 3*m1));
pair p3 = (36/(3 + m2), 4*m2 + 36/(3 + m2));

draw((xMin,0)--(xMax,0),black+linewidth(1.5),EndArrow(5));
draw((0,yMin)--(0,yMax),black+linewidth(1.5),EndArrow(5));
draw(reflect((0,0),(1,1))*(y == m1*x + b1), blue+linewidth(1.5));
draw(reflect((0,0),(1,1))*((y == -m1*x + b1)), blue+linewidth(1.5));
draw((x + 3*y == 12), red+linewidth(1.5));

label("$y = x + 4$", (6.25, 10), blue);
label("$y = -x + 4$", (5.25, 1.25), blue);
label("$x + 3y = 12$", (5.5, 6), red);

dot(p1, red);
dot(p2, red);
dot(p3, red);

label("$A$", p1, NW);
label("$B$", p2, SE);
label("$C$", p3, S);
[/asy]

We can see that $A = (0,4)$ and $B$ lies on the line $x + 3y = 12$. Substituting $y = x+4$ into the equation for the line, we get $x + 3(x+4) = 12$, so $x = -\frac{12}{4} = -3$ and $y = 1$. Therefore, $B = (-3,1)$. $C$ is the intersection of lines $y = x + 4$ and $x + 3y = 12$. Substituting $y = x+4$ into the equation for the second line, we get $x + 3(x+4) = 12$, so $x = -\frac{12}{4} = -3$ and $y = 1$. Therefore, $C = (-3,1)$.

We can see that the triangle is degenerate, since all three vertices lie on the same line $y = x+4$. Therefore, the area of the triangle is $\boxed{0}$ square units.

Let the perpendiculars from $C$ and $D$ to $BE$ intersect $BE$ at $X$ and $Y$, respectively.  These perpendiculars split trapezoid $BCDE$ into two isosceles right triangles $\triangle BCX$ and $\triangle EDY$ and one rectangle $CDYX$.

In isosceles right triangles (which have angles 45-45-90), the ratio of the leg length to the hypotenuse length is $1:\sqrt{2}$; hence, we have $BX=CX=DY=YE=12/\sqrt{2}=6\sqrt{2}$.  We also have $XY=CD=12$, as opposite sides of a rectangle are equal.

Thus, trapezoid $BCDE$ has bases of length $CD=12$ and $BE=6\sqrt{2}+12+6\sqrt{2}=12+12\sqrt{2}$, and height of length $6\sqrt{2}$.  Hence its area is $\frac{1}{2}(12 + 12 + 12\sqrt{2}) (6\sqrt{2}) = \boxed{72 + 72\sqrt{2}}$.

Since $ABCDEFGH$ is a regular octagon, all of its sides are congruent. We are given that each of its sides have length $12$ cm. We note also that trapezoid $BCDE$ has bases of length $12$ cm and $24$ cm since $BC=DE=12$ cm and $CD=BE=24$ cm. The height of trapezoid $BCDE$ is equal to the length of side $AF$, which is also equal to the length of the apothem of regular octagon $ABCDEFGH$. We label this length as $s$.

[asy] real x = 22.5; draw(dir(0+x)--dir(45+x)--dir(90+x)--dir(90+45+x)--

dir(90+2*45+x)--dir(90+3*45+x)-- dir(90+4*45+x)-- dir(90+5*45+x)--dir(90+6*45+x));

label("$A$", dir(90+45+x), W); label("$B$", dir(90+x), NW);label("$C$", dir(45+x), NE); label("$D$", dir(x), E);label("$E$", dir(90+5*45+x), E);label("$F$", dir(90+4*45+x), SE); label("$G$", dir(90+3*45+x), SW);label("$H$", dir(90+2*45+x), W);
draw( dir(90+x)--dir(90+5*45+x) ); draw( dir(90+x)--dir(45+x) );

label("$s$",(dir(45+x)+dir(90+x))/2,NW); label("$s$",(dir(90+x)+dir(90+45+x))/2,N); label("$12$",(dir(45+x)+dir(x))/2,NE); label("$24$",(dir(90+x)+dir(x))/2,S); label("$12$",(dir(90+5*45+x)+dir(90+4*45+x))/2,SE); label("$24$",(dir(90+3*45+x)+dir(90+4*45+x))/2,S);[/asy]

To find $s$, we first draw the line segment $AG$, which is a diagonal of the regular octagon. This diagonal divides regular octagon $ABCDEFGH$ into congruent isosceles triangles $\triangle AFG$ and $\triangle ABG$. Then, we focus on $\triangle AFG$ and use the Pythagorean Theorem to find the length of $AG$, which is equal to twice the length of $s$. We have:

$$AG^2 = AF^2 + FG^2 = 12^2 + s^2 \implies AG = 2s\sqrt{4 + \frac{1}{3}} = \frac{8s}{\sqrt{3}}$$Therefore, $s = \frac{AG}{2} \cdot \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4} = \frac{12}{\sqrt{3}} = 4\sqrt{3}$ cm.

The area of trapezoid $BCDE$ is $\frac{(BC + DE)h}{2}$, where $h$ is the height of the trapezoid. We found that the height of the trapezoid is equal to $s=4\sqrt{3}$ cm. Thus, we have:

\begin{align*}
\frac{(BC + DE)h}{2} &= \frac{(12+24)(4\sqrt{3})}{2} \\
&= 36\sqrt{3} \\
\end{align*}

Therefore, the area of trapezoid $BCDE$ is $\boxed{36\sqrt{3}}$ square centimeters.

First we find $\angle ACB$ by adding up the three angles in the triangle: \[\angle ACB = 180^\circ - \angle BAC - \angle ABC = 180^\circ - 21^\circ - 45^\circ = 114^\circ.\]Since $CX$ bisects $\angle ACB$, we have $\angle ACX = \angle XCB = 57^\circ$. Now consider right triangle $ACX$: \[\tan(57^\circ) = \frac{AX}{21} \qquad \implies \qquad AX = 21\tan(57^\circ) \approx \boxed{36}.\]Notice that we can obtain $\tan(57^\circ)$ using the fact that $57^\circ + 33^\circ = 90^\circ$ and taking the tangent of both sides: \[\tan(57^\circ) = \tan(90^\circ - 33^\circ) = \frac{1}{\tan(33^\circ)}.\]We recognize $\tan(33^\circ)$; this is a well-known value (often derived using the golden ratio), and we have \[\tan(33^\circ) = \frac{\cos(57^\circ)}{\sin(57^\circ)} = \frac{1}{\tan(57^\circ) + 1}.\]Solving for $\tan(57^\circ)$ yields $\tan(57^\circ) \approx 1.54$, and then multiplying by 21 gives $AX \approx 21\cdot 1.54 \approx \boxed{36}$

First, we look at $\triangle MDN.$ We know that $DM = 4,$ $DN=2,$ and $\angle MDN = 60^\circ$ (because $\triangle EDF$ is equilateral).  Since $DM:DN=2:1$ and the contained angle is $60^\circ,$ $\triangle MDN$ is a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. Therefore, $MN$ is perpendicular to $DF,$ and $MN =\sqrt{3}DN = 2\sqrt{3}.$

Next, we calculate $CP.$ We know that $QC = 8$ and $\angle QCP = 60^\circ.$ Since $MN\perp DF,$ plane $MNPQ$ is perpendicular to plane $BCDF.$ Since $QP || MN$ (they lie in the same plane $MNPQ$ and in parallel planes $ACB$ and $DEF$), $QP \perp CB.$

Therefore, $\triangle QCP$ is right-angled at $P$ and contains a $60^\circ$ angle, so is also a $30^\circ$-$60^\circ$-$90^\circ$ triangle. It follows that $$CP = \frac{1}{2}(CQ)=\frac{1}{2}(8)=4$$and $QP = \sqrt{3} CP = 4\sqrt{3}.$

Then, we construct. We extend $CD$ downwards and extend $QM$ until it intersects the extension of $CD$ at $R.$ (Note here that the line through $QM$ will intersect the line through $CD$ since they are two non-parallel lines lying in the same plane.) [asy]

[/asy] Finally, we calculate the volume of $QPCDMN.$ The volume of $QPCDMN$ equals the difference between the volume of the triangular -based pyramid $RCQP$ and the volume of the triangular-based pyramid $RDMN.$

We have  \[ [\triangle CPQ]=\frac{1}{2}(CP)(QP)=\frac{1}{2}(4)(4\sqrt{3})=8\sqrt{3}\]and  \[ [\triangle DNM] =\frac{1}{2}(DN)(MN)=\frac{1}{2}(2)(2\sqrt{3})=2\sqrt{3}.\]The volume of a tetrahedron equals one-third times the area of the base times the height. We have $RD=16$ and $RC=32.$ Therefore, the volume of $QPCDMN$ is \[\frac{1}{3}(8\sqrt{3})(32)-\frac{1}{3}(2\sqrt{3})(16)=\frac{256\sqrt{3}}{3} - \frac{32\sqrt{3}}{3}=\boxed{\frac{224\sqrt{3}}{3}}.\]

For a circle of radius $r$ and an arc of $\theta$ degrees, the arc length is $(2\pi r)\frac{\theta}{360}$. Thus, for the same arc length, the arc angle is inversely proportional to the radius, so the ratio of the radius of circle $A$ to the radius of circle $B$ is $40:55$, or $8:11$. Since the ratio of the areas of two circles is the square of the ratio of their radii, the ratio of the area of circle $A$ to the area of circle $B$ is $(8/11)^2=\boxed{\frac{64}{121}}$.

Let $r_{A}$ and $r_{B}$ represent the radii of circle $A$ and circle $B$, respectively.  Let $L_{A}$ and $L_{B}$ represent the lengths of the arcs described in the problem.  Circle $A$ has circumference $2\pi r_{A}$, so $$\frac{55}{360}(2\pi r_{A})=L_{A}.$$Circle $B$ has circumference $2\pi r_{B}$, so $$\frac{40}{360}(2\pi r_{B})=L_{B}.$$We know that $L_{A}=L_{B}$, so we have $$\frac{55}{360}(2\pi r_{A})=\frac{40}{360}(2\pi r_{B}).$$Simplifying the equation yields $$\frac{r_{A}}{r_{B}}=\frac{40}{55}=\frac{8}{11}.$$Therefore, the ratio of the area of circle $A$ to the area of circle $B$ is $$\left(\frac{r_{A}}{r_{B}}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{8}{11}\right)^{2}=\boxed{\frac{64}{121}}.$$

[/asy] It is clear that we have a $3:4:5$ right triangle on our hands, so we have a right angle at $B.$ That means if we can find $DB,$ we can use the Pythagorean Theorem on $\triangle ABD$ to find $AD^2.$

To find $DB,$ we see that $DB:DC = AB:AC = 6:10$ thanks to the Angle Bisector Theorem. That means $DB:DC:BC = 6:10:16,$ since $BC = DB + DC.$ So $DB = \frac{6}{16} \cdot BC = 3.$

Finally, we have that $AD^2 = AB^2 + BD^2 = 6^2 + 3^2 = 36 + 9 = \boxed{45}.$

We begin by drawing a diagram: [asy]
pair A,B,C,D;
A = (-2,0);
B = (4,0);

label("$B$",B,SE);
label("$C$",C,N);
label("$D$",D,S);

[/asy] By the angle bisector theorem, we have $$\frac{BD}{DC} = \frac{AB}{AC} = \frac 35.$$ Thus, letting $BD = 3x$ and $DC = 5x,$ we have $BC = 8x = 8$ and so $x = 1.$ Thus, $BD = 3$ and $DC = 5.$

We apply the angle bisector theorem again to find $$\frac{AD}{DB} = \frac{CA}{CB} = \frac 54.$$ Therefore, letting $AD = 5y$ and $DB = 3y,$ we find $AB = 6 = AD + DB = 8y$ and so $y = \frac 34.$ Thus, $AD = \frac {15}4$ and $$AD^2 = \left(\frac {15}4\right)^2 = \boxed{\frac {225}{16}}.$$

Triangles $AXC$ and $ABC$ share an altitude from $C$, so \[\frac{[AXC]}{[ABC]} = \frac{AX}{AB} =\frac47.\] Therefore, $[AXC] = \frac47[ABC]$.

Triangles $AXY$ and $AXC$ share an altitude from $X$, so \[\frac{[AXY]}{[AXC]}=\frac{AY}{AC} = \frac{6}{10} = \frac35.\] Therefore, $[AXY] = \frac35[AXC]$, so \[[AXY] = \frac35[AXC] = \frac35\cdot \frac47[ABC] = \frac{12}{35}[ABC],\] which means that the desired ratio is $\boxed{\frac{12}{35}}$.

We start with the formula for the area of a triangle given its side lengths. Let $K$ be the area of triangle $ABC$. Then
\[K=\frac{1}{2}(AB)(AC)\sin A.\]Let $L$ be the area of triangle $AXY$. The key observation is that $L$ is the average value of $K$ as point $X$ moves along $AB$.

To see this, let $f(x)$ denote the area of the triangle $x$ units to the right of point $A$ on $\overline{AB}$. Then $f$ is a linear function of $x$. Furthermore, $f(0)=0$, $f(7)=K$, and $f(4)=L$. Therefore,
\[L=\frac{f(4)+f(0)}{2},\]which simplifies to $L=\frac{K}{2}$.

[asy]
unitsize(0.3 cm);

pair A, B, C, X, Y;

A = (0,0);
B = (7,0);
C = (3,16);
X = (4*B + 3*A)/7;
Y = (6*C + 4*A)/10;

draw(A--B--C--cycle);
draw(A--X--Y--cycle);
draw(rightanglemark(B,X,A,20));
draw(rightanglemark(C,Y,A,20));

label("$A$", A, SW);
label("$B$", B, SE);
label("$C$", C, N);
label("$X$", X, S);
label("$Y$", Y, NW);
label("$7$", (A + B)/2, S);
label("$10$", (A + C)/2, W);
label("$4$", (A + X)/2, S);
label("$6$", (A + Y)/2, W);
[/asy]

It follows that the ratio of $L$ to $K$ is $\boxed{\frac{1}{2}}$.

Let the radius of the large semicircle be $6x$.  The diameter of the smallest semicircle is $\frac{1}{1+2+3} = \frac16$ of the diameter of the largest semicircle, so the radius of the smallest semicircle is $x$.  Similarly, the radius of the next smallest semicircle is $2x$, and the radius of the next semicircle is $3x$.  The unshaded area then is the sum of the areas of the three smallest semicircles: \[\frac12(x)^2\pi + \frac12 (2x)^2 \pi + \frac12(3x)^2\pi = \frac12(x^2 + 4x^2 + 9x^2)\pi = (7x^2)\pi.\] The largest semicircle has area $\frac12(6x)^2\pi = 18x^2\pi$, so the shaded area is \[18x^2\pi - 7x^2 \pi = 11x^2\pi.\] Therefore, the desired ratio is \[\frac{11x^2\pi}{7x^2\pi} = \boxed{\frac{11}{7}}.\]

Let $A$ be the center of the largest semicircle, and let $a$ be the radius of the largest semicircle.  Then $B$, $C$, and $D$ are the centers, respectively, of semicircles with diameters $AB$, $BC$, and $CD$.  Their radii are $\frac{a}{2}$, $\frac{3a}{2}$, and $\frac{9a}{2}$. Therefore, the shaded area has ratio $$\frac{\frac{1}{2}\pi a^2 - \frac{1}{6}\cdot\frac{1}{2}\pi a^2}{\frac{1}{2}\pi a^2 - \frac{1}{4}\cdot\frac{1}{2}\pi (\frac{9a}{2})^2} = \frac{5}{16}.$$

P.J.'s juice fills up a cylinder with radius 3/2 inches and height 6 inches; his juice has volume $\pi \left(\frac{3}{2}\right)^2(6)= \frac{27\pi}{2}$ cubic inches.

Schuyler's juice fills up a cylinder with radius 2 inches and unknown height $h$ inches; his juice has volume $\pi (2^2) (h) = 4\pi h$ cubic inches.  Setting this equal to P.J.'s volume and solving for $h$ yields $\frac{27\pi}{2} = 4\pi h \Rightarrow h = \frac{27}{8}= \boxed{3 \frac{3}{8}}$ inches.

Extracting Answer from: 
The volume of P.J.'s mug can be calculated using the formula for the volume of a cylinder: V = πr^2h, where r is the radius (half the diameter) and h is the height. P.J.'s mug has a diameter of 3 inches, so its radius is 3/2 = 1.5 inches. P.J.'s mug contains V = π(1.5)^2 × 6 ≈ 42.4 cubic inches of juice.

To find how high Schuyler's mug must be filled, we can use the same formula. Schuyler's mug has a diameter of 4 inches, so its radius is 2 inches. We want to find the height h such that the volume of Schuyler's mug is also about 42.4 cubic inches: π(2)^2h ≈ 42.4. Dividing both sides by 4π, we get h ≈ 42.4/(4π(2)^2) ≈ 1.34.

Therefore, Schuyler's mug must be filled to a height of about 1 and 1/3 inches to contain the same amount of juice as P.J.'s mug.

Extracting Answer from: 
We know where points $A$, $B$, and $C$ are, and we know that the shape is a parallelogram, so we know that the differences in the x-values and y-values between $B$ and $A$ must be the same as the differences in x-values and y-values between $C$ and $D$.

The solution is $\boxed{(-5, -4)}$, which keeps the vertices in alphabetical order, as shown in the first figure. Two other parallelograms are shown in figures 2 and 3 (where we number them from left to right), but they are not solutions because the points aren't in the correct order. They would be parallelograms $ACBD$ and $ABDC$, respectively. [asy]
import olympiad; import geometry; size(250); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8));
picture a,b,c;
xaxis(a,YZero(),-6,6,Ticks(beginlabel=false,Step=20,step=2));
yaxis(a,XZero(),-5,13,Ticks(beginlabel=false,Step=20,step=2));
xaxis(b,YZero(),-6,6,Ticks(beginlabel=false,Step=20,step=2));
yaxis(b,XZero(),-5,13,Ticks(beginlabel=false,Step=20,step=2));
xaxis(c,YZero(),-6,17,Ticks(beginlabel=false,Step=10,step=2));
yaxis(c,XZero(),-5,15,Ticks(beginlabel=false,Step=10,step=2));
pair A = (-3,5),B=(7,12),C=(5,3);
draw(a,A--B--C--(-5,-4)--cycle);
label(a,"$A$",A,NW); label(a,"$B$",B,NE); label(a,"$C$",C,SE); label(a,"$D$",(-5,-4),SW);
draw(b,A--C--B--(-1,14)--cycle);
label(b,"$A$",A,SW); label(b,"$B$",B,NE); label(b,"$C$",C,SE); label(b,"$D$",(-1,14),NW);
draw(c,A--C--(15,10)--B--cycle);
label(c,"$A$",A,W); label(c,"$B$",B,N); label(c,"$C$",C,S); label(c,"$D$",(15,10),E);
add(currentpicture,a);
add(currentpicture, shift(20,0)*b);

Since opposite sides of a parallelogram are parallel, then the vector $\overrightarrow{AB}$ is equal to the vector $\overrightarrow{DC}$. Also, since $\overrightarrow{AB} = \langle 7 - (-3), 12 - 5\rangle = \langle 10, 7\rangle$ then $\overrightarrow{DC} = \langle 10, 7\rangle$. Starting from point $C(5, 3)$ and moving in the direction of this vector will give us the coordinates of point $D$. Thus, $D = \boxed{(15, 10)}$.

Let $r$ be the number of inches in the radius of the can.  The volume of the can is $\pi r^2(\text{height})=6\pi r^2$ cubic inches.  The lateral surface area is $2\pi r (\text{height})=12\pi r$ square inches.  Setting $6\pi r^2$ equal to $12\pi r$, we find  \begin{align*}
6\pi r^2-12\pi r&=0 \implies \\
6\pi r( r-2 )&=0 \implies \\
r=0 \quad &\text{or}\quad r=2.
\end{align*} Taking the positive solution $r=2$, we find that the radius of the can is $\boxed{2}$ inches.

Now let's set up an equation using the information given in the problem: V = 6πr^2

We know that the height of the cylinder is 6 inches, so we can substitute that in for h: V = πr^2(6)

We also know that the area of the label on the lateral surface of the can is equal to the volume of the can. So the area of the label is also 6πr^2 square inches.

The lateral surface of a cylinder is the curved surface, excluding the top and bottom of the can. The formula for the lateral surface area of a cylinder is A = 2πrh, where A is the lateral surface area.

So we can set up another equation using the lateral surface area: A = 2πrh

Since the height of the can is 6 inches, we can substitute that in for h: A = 2πr(6)

We are now given that the area of the label on the lateral surface of the can is equal to the volume of the can. So we can set the two equations equal to each other: 

6πr^2 = 2πr(6)

Simplifying the right side of the equation gives:

6πr^2=12πr

Dividing both sides by 6πr gives:

r=2

Therefore, the radius of the can is 2 inches.

Since $\sin (180^\circ - x) =\sin x$ and $\cos (180^\circ - x) = -\cos x$ for any angle, we have $$\tan(180^\circ - x) =
\frac{\sin(180^\circ - x)}{\cos(180^\circ - x)} = \frac{\sin x}{-\cos x} = -\tan x$$for any angle for which $\tan x$ is defined.  Therefore, $\tan\angle BDC = -\tan\angle BDA$.

From the Pythagorean Theorem, we have $AC = \sqrt{BC^2 - BA^2} = 24$.  Since $D$ is the midpoint of $\overline{AC}$, we have $AD = AC/2 =12$.  Therefore, we have $\tan \angle BDC = -\tan \angle BDA = -\frac{BA}{AD} = \boxed{-\frac{7}{12}}$.

Let $E$ be the foot of the altitude from $B$ to $\overline{AC}$, and let $x = DE = EC$.  Note that $\triangle ABE$ and $\triangle BCD$ are similar right triangles.  Also, $\triangle BAE$ is a $7$-$24$-$25$ right triangle, so $\sin \angle ABE = \frac{24}{25}$ and $\cos \angle ABE = \frac{7}{25}$. Therefore, we have $\tan \angle ABE = \frac{24}{7}$. Using similarity, we have $BD = 2x$ and $CD = x + 25$. Thus, $\tan \angle BDC = \tan (\angle ABE + \angle ABD + \angle CBD) = \frac{\tan \angle ABE + \tan \angle ABD + \tan \angle CBD - \tan \angle ABE \tan \angle ABD \tan \angle CBD}{1 - \tan \angle ABE \tan \angle ABD - \tan \angle ABE \tan \angle CBD - \tan \angle ABD \tan \angle CBD}.$

Since $BD = 2x$ and $DC = x + 25$, we have $\tan \angle ABD = \frac{BE}{BD} = \frac{BE}{2x}$ and $\tan \angle CBD = \frac{BE}{DC} = \frac{BE}{x+25}$.  Note that $\triangle BBE \sim \triangle BCD$, so $\frac{BE}{BC} = \frac{BBE}{BCD} = \frac{AE}{CD}$.  We can solve for $AE$ using the Pythagorean Theorem in $\triangle ABE$: $AE = \sqrt{AB^2 - BE^2} = \sqrt{7^2 - \left(\frac{24}{25} \cdot BC\right)^2} = \frac{77}{5}$, so $\frac{BE}{BC} = \frac{77}{125}$.  Solving for $\tan \angle ABD$ and $\tan \angle CBD$, we get $\tan \angle ABD = \frac{24}{154}$ and $\tan \angle CBD = \frac{24}{x+25}$.

Therefore, we have $\tan \angle BDC = \frac{\frac{24}{7} + \frac{24}{154} + \frac{24}{x+25} - \frac{24}{7} \cdot \frac{24}{154} \cdot \frac{24}{x+25}}{1 - \frac{24}{7} \cdot \frac{24}{154} - \frac{24}{7} \cdot \frac{24}{x+25} - \frac{24}{154} \cdot \frac{24}{x+25}}$.  Simplifying the numerator and denominator separately, we get:

Numerator: $\frac{4325x+104880}{539x+36960}$

Denominator: $-\frac{576}{539x+36960}$

Therefore, $\tan \angle BDC = \frac{4325x+104880}{-576(539x+36960)} = -\frac{25x+610}{192(539x+36960)}.$  Since $\triangle ABD \sim \triangle BDC$, we have $\frac{AB}{AD} = \frac{BD}{DC}$, so $\frac{7}{25-x} = \frac{2x}{x+25}$, which we can solve to get $x = \frac{625}{34}$.  Substituting, we get $\tan \angle BDC = -\frac{25 \cdot \frac{625}{34} + 610}{192 \cdot (539 \cdot \frac{625}{34} + 36960)} = \boxed{-\frac{1009}{66903}}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Let's use the formula for the volume of a cube:

V = side³

We are given that the initial cube has a volume of:

V1 = 2³ = 8 cubic centimeters

We are also given that the volume of the new cube is 19 cubic centimeters more than the initial cube:

V2 = V1 + 19
V2 = 8 + 19
V2 = 27 cubic centimeters

To find the length of one edge of the new cube, we need to calculate the side length (s) of the cube using the formula:

s = ∛V

where ∛ is the cube root.

So for the new cube:

s = ∛V2
s = ∛27
s = 3

Therefore, the length of one edge of the new cube is 3 centimeters.

There are many ways to draw the diagram; one possibility is shown below.  We know that $\angle BAC \cong \angle CAD\cong \angle BCA$ since $\overline{AC}$ bisects $\angle BAD$ and $\overline{AD} || \overline{BC}$.  Therefore $\triangle BAC$ is isosceles.  In the diagram we have added segment $\overline{BE}$ splitting $\triangle BAC$ into two smaller congruent right triangles.  We also know that $\triangle ACD$ is a right triangle by the givens, so we conclude that $\triangle ACD \sim \triangle CEB$ since we already know that $\angle CAD\cong\angle ECB$.  In fact, $\triangle ACD $ is exactly four times the size of $\triangle CEB$ since $AC=2(EC)$.  If we let $[\triangle CEB]=K$, then $[\triangle AEB]=K$ while $[\triangle ACD]=4K$.  Thus $6K=42$, so $K=7$ and $[\triangle ACD]=4K=\boxed{28}$.

[asy]
import olympiad; import graph; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); dotfactor=4;
int randangle = 50;
draw((-5,0)--(5*dir(randangle))--(5,0)--cycle);
path x1 = (5*Cos(randangle),5*Sin(randangle))--(-10,5*Sin(randangle));
path x2 = (-5,0)--(5dir(2*randangle));
pair X = intersectionpoint(x1,x2);
draw((-5,0)--X--(5*dir(randangle)));
draw(rightanglemark((5,0),(5*dir(50)),(-5,0),s=14));
draw(anglemark((5,0),(-5,0),X,18));
draw(anglemark(X,5*dir(50),(-5,0),18));
label("$A$",(-5,0),W); label("$D$",(5,0),E); label("$C$",(5*dir(50)),E);
label("$B$",(X),N);

pair L = foot(X,(-5,0),5*dir(50));
draw(X--L);
draw(rightanglemark(X,L,(-5,0),14)); draw(rightanglemark(X,L,(5*dir(50)),14));
label("$E$",L,SE);
[/asy]

[asy]
size(8cm);
pair A = (-8,8);
pair D = (8,8);
pair B = (-2,-8);

pair CD = C--D;

Since $\overline{AC}$ bisects $\angle BAD,$ we have $$\angle BAP = \angle PAC = \angle CAD.$$Therefore, $\triangle PAC \sim \triangle ACD,$ so \begin{align*}
\frac{AC}{CD} &= \frac{AP}{AD}\\
\Rightarrow\qquad AC &= \frac{AP}{AD} \cdot CD.
\end{align*}Letting $[XYZ]$ denote the area of $\triangle XYZ,$ we then have \begin{align*}
[ACD]&= \frac{1}{2} \cdot AC \cdot CD\\
&= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{AP}{AD} \cdot CD^2\\
&= \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{AP}{AD} \cdot \frac{AD^2+AB^2-2 \cdot AD \cdot AB \cdot \cos \angle BAD}{2}\\
&= \frac{AP}{4} \cdot \left(\frac{AD}{\sin \angle BAD}\right) \cdot [\triangle ABD]\\
&= \frac{AP}{4} \cdot BD \cdot \sin \angle ABD \cdot \frac{AB+BD}{2}\\
&= \frac{AP}{8}\cdot AB \cdot BD^2 \cdot \sin \angle ABD.
\end{align*} Note that $[ABCD] = \frac{AB+CD}{2}\cdot AC = 42$ and $AM = AC/2$, so $$42 = \frac{AB+CD}{2}\cdot AM,$$or equivalently, $$AB+CD = \frac{84}{AM}.$$We can then solve for $BD = AB - CD$ and $AD$ in terms of $AM$: $$BD = \frac{84}{2AM} - AM, \qquad AD = AM \sqrt{2^2 + \left(\frac{84}{2AM} - AM\right)^2}.$$We then have \begin{align*}
[\triangle ACD] &= [ABCD] - [\triangle BCD] - [\triangle ABC]\\
&= 42 - \frac{1}{2} (CD+BD) \cdot AC - \frac{1}{2}(AB+BD)\cdot AC\\
&= 42 -\frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{84}{AM} \left(\frac{AP \cdot CD}{AD} + \frac{AP \cdot AB}{AD}\right)\\
&= 42-\frac{1}{2} \cdot AP \cdot \frac{84}{AM^2} \cdot \frac{AC}{AD}\cdot (AB+CD)\\
&= 42-21\left(\frac{AP}{AM\cdot AD}\right) \left(\frac{AB+CD}{AM}\right) \cdot AC\\
&= 42-21\left(\frac{AP}{AM\cdot AD}\right) \cdot \left(2AM\right) \cdot AC\\
&= 42-42 \cdot \left(\frac{AP}{AD}\right)^2\\
&= \boxed{006}.
\end{align*}Notice that we have solved entirely for $[\triangle ACD]$ based on information involving $\triangle ABP.$  This is what makes the problem especially elegant:  we can use similarity to completely ignore $\triangle ABC,$ $\triangle BCD,$ and $BD.$

We know that $OA$ and $OB$ are each radii of the semi-circle with center $O$.  Thus, $OA=OB=OC+CB=32+36=68$.  Therefore, $AC=AO+OC=68+32=100$.

The semi-circle with center $K$ has radius $AK=\frac{1}{2}(AC)=\frac{1}{2}(100)=50$.  Thus, this semi-circle has an area equal to $\frac{1}{2}\pi(AK)^2=\frac{1}{2}\pi(50)^2=\boxed{1250\pi}$.

Extracting Answer from: 
Angles $BAC$ and $BCA$ are each inscribed angles, so each one is equal to half of the measure of the arc they subtend. Therefore, the measures of arcs $AB$ and $BC$ are each 70 degrees, and together, the measure of arc $ABC$ is 140 degrees. Notice that angle $CDA$ is also an inscribed angle, and it subtends arc $ABC$, so $m\angle CDA = \frac{1}{2} (\text{arc } ABC) = (1/2)(140) = \boxed{70}$ degrees.

GPT File: datasets/4-20-total500qs/geometry/answers/986_answer.json
Extracting Answer from: 
The angles of $\triangle ABC$ sum to $180^\circ$, so we have $$\angle ABC + \angle BAC + \angle BCA = 180^\circ.$$Since $\triangle ABC$ is isosceles, $\angle ABC = \angle BCA$. Substituting, we have $$2\angle BCA + 35^\circ = 180^\circ,$$so $\angle BCA = 72.5^\circ$.

[asy]
import olympiad; import geometry; size(125); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8));
draw(circle((0,0),1));
dot("$F$",(0,0),dir(-90));
pair A = dir(160), B = dir(110), C = dir(40), D = dir(10);
draw(A--D--C--A--B--C);
label("$A$",A,W); label("$B$",B,N); label("$C$",C,E); label("$D$",D,E);
label("$72.5^\circ$",(1/3*(A+B) + 2/3*C),NW);
[/asy]

In cyclic quadrilateral $ABCD$, we have the opposite angles of $\angle ABC$ and $\angle ADC$ summing to $180^\circ$. Since $\angle ABC = \angle BCA$, we have $$\angle ADC = \angle BCD = 180^\circ - \angle ABC = 180^\circ - 72.5^\circ = 107.5^\circ.$$Therefore, the measure of $\angle CDA$ is $$180^\circ - \angle ADC = 180^\circ - 107.5^\circ = \boxed{72.5^\circ}.$$
